<p><b>Amartya Kumar Sen</b> (pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ˈɔmort:o ˈʃen</span>; born 3 November 1933) is an <a href="/wiki/Indian_people" title="Indian people">Indian</a> economist and philosopher of Bengali ethnicity, who since 1972 has taught and worked in the United Kingdom and the United States. Sen has made contributions to <a href="/wiki/Welfare_economics" title="Welfare economics">welfare economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_choice_theory" title="Social choice theory">social choice theory</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Economic_justice" title="Economic justice">economic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a>, economic theories of <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famines</a>, and indexes of the measure of well-being of citizens of developing countries. He was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Memorial_Prize_in_Economic_Sciences" title="Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences">Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences</a> in 1998 and <a href="/wiki/Bharat_Ratna" title="Bharat Ratna">Bharat Ratna</a> in 1999 for his work in welfare economics.</p>
<p>He was also awarded the inaugural Charleston-EFG John Maynard Keynes Prize in recognition of his work on <a href="/wiki/Welfare_economics" title="Welfare economics">welfare economics</a> in February 2015 during a reception at the Royal Academy in the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> He served as the <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_(education)" title="Chancellor (education)">Chancellor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nalanda_University" title="Nalanda University">Nalanda University</a>.</p>
<p>He is currently the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Professor" title="Harvard University Professor">Thomas W. Lamont University Professor</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_Harvard_DP_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_Harvard_DP-5">[5]</a></sup> and Professor of Economics and Philosophy at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_Harvard_DP_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_Harvard_DP-5">[5]</a></sup> He also serves in the faculty of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Law_School" title="Harvard Law School">Harvard Law School</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_HLS_Harvard_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_HLS_Harvard-6">[6]</a></sup> He is also a senior fellow at the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Society_of_Fellows" title="Harvard Society of Fellows">Harvard Society of Fellows</a>, a distinguished fellow of <a href="/wiki/All_Souls_College,_Oxford" title="All Souls College, Oxford">All Souls College, Oxford</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_All_Souls_Oxford_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_All_Souls_Oxford-7">[7]</a></sup> an honorary fellow of <a href="/wiki/Darwin_College,_Cambridge" title="Darwin College, Cambridge">Darwin College, Cambridge</a> and a Fellow of <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, Cambridge</a>, where he served as Master from 1998 to 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Master_Camb_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Master_Camb-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amartya_Sen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sen was born in a Bengali brahmin family in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manikganj" title="Manikganj">Manikganj</a> (in <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British India</a>, now <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>), to Ashutosh Sen and Amita Sen. <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Rabindranath Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a> gave Amartya Sen his name (Bengali অমর্ত্য <i>ômorto</i>, lit. "immortal"). Sen's family was from Wari and <a href="/wiki/Manikganj_District" title="Manikganj District">Manikganj</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a>, both in present-day <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>. His father Ashutosh Sen was a professor of chemistry at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhaka_University" title="Dhaka University">Dhaka University</a> who moved with his family to <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a> in 1945 and worked at various government institutions, including the West Bengal Public Service Commission (of which he was the chairman), and the <a href="/wiki/Union_Public_Service_Commission" title="Union Public Service Commission">Union Public Service Commission</a>. Sen's mother Amita Sen was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Kshitimohan_Sen" title="Kshitimohan Sen">Kshiti Mohan Sen</a>, a well-known scholar of ancient and medieval India and close associate of Rabindranath Tagore. He served as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vice_Chancellor" title="Vice Chancellor">Vice Chancellor</a> of <a href="/wiki/Visva-Bharati_University" title="Visva-Bharati University">Visva-Bharati University</a> for some years.</p>
<p>Sen began his high-school education at <a href="/wiki/St_Gregory%27s_School_(Dhaka)" title="St Gregory's School (Dhaka)">St Gregory's School</a> in Dhaka in 1940. From fall 1941, Sen studied at <a href="/wiki/Patha_Bhavana" title="Patha Bhavana">Patha Bhavana</a>, Santiniketan. The school had many progressive features: at the school, any focus on examinations or competitive testing was deeply frowned upon. In addition, the school stressed cultural diversity, and embraced influences from the rest of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> In 1951, he went to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presidency_College,_Kolkata" title="Presidency College, Kolkata">Presidency College, Kolkata</a>, where he earned a B.A. in Economics with First Class, with a minor in Mathematics, as a graduating student of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Calcutta" title="University of Calcutta">University of Calcutta</a>. While at Presidency, Sen was diagnosed with oral cancer, and given a 15% chance of living five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> With radiation treatment, he survived, and in 1953 he moved to <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, Cambridge</a>, where he earned a second B.A. in Pure Economics in 1955 with a First Class, topping the list as well. He was elected President of the Cambridge Majlis. While Sen was officially a Ph.D student at Cambridge (though he had finished his research in 1955-6), he was offered the position of Professor and Head of the Economics Department of the newly created <a href="/wiki/Jadavpur_University" title="Jadavpur University">Jadavpur University</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcutta" title="Calcutta">Calcutta</a>, and he became the youngest chairman to head the Department of Economics. He served in that position, starting the new Economics Department, during 1956 to 1958.</p>
<p>Meanwhile, Sen was elected to a Prize Fellowship at Trinity College, which gave him four years of freedom to do anything he liked; he made the radical decision to study <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>. Sen explained: "The broadening of my studies into philosophy was important for me not just because some of my main areas of interest in economics relate quite closely to philosophical disciplines (for example, <a href="/wiki/Social_choice_theory" title="Social choice theory">social choice theory</a> makes intense use of <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">mathematical logic</a> and also draws on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moral_philosophy" title="Moral philosophy">moral philosophy</a>, and so does the study of inequality and deprivation), but also because I found philosophical studies very rewarding on their own".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Philosophy_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Philosophy-11">[11]</a></sup> His interest in philosophy, however, dates back to his college days at Presidency, where he read books on philosophy and debated philosophical themes.</p>
<p>In Cambridge, there were major debates between supporters of <a href="/wiki/Keynesian_economics" title="Keynesian economics">Keynesian economics</a> on the one hand, and the "neo-classical" economists skeptical of Keynes, on the other. However, because of a lack of enthusiasm for social choice theory in both Trinity and Cambridge, Sen had to choose a different subject for his <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy" title="Doctor of Philosophy">Ph.D.</a> thesis, which was on "<a href="/wiki/Choice_of_techniques" title="Choice of techniques">The Choice of Techniques</a>" in 1959, though the work had been completed much earlier (except for some valuable advice from his adjunct supervisor in India, Professor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.K._Dasgupta" title="A.K. Dasgupta">A.K. Dasgupta</a>, given to Sen while teaching and revising his work at Jadavpur) under the supervision of the "brilliant but vigorously intolerant" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-Keynesian" title="Post-Keynesian">post-Keynesian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joan_Robinson" title="Joan Robinson">Joan Robinson</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Robinson-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Quentin_Skinner" title="Quentin Skinner">Quentin Skinner</a> notes that Sen was a member of the secret society <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Apostles" title="Cambridge Apostles">Cambridge Apostles</a> during his time at Cambridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_Work">Research Work</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amartya_Sen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Research Work">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sen's work on 'Choice of Technique' complemented that of Maurice Dobb. In a Developing country, the Dobb-Sen strategy relied on maximising investible surpluses, maintaining constant real wages and using the entire increase in labour productivity, due to technological change, to raise the rate of accumulation. In other words, workers were expected to demand no improvement in their standard of living despite having become more productive. Sen's papers in the late 1960s and early 1970s helped develop the theory of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_choice" title="Social choice">social choice</a>, which first came to prominence in the work by the American economist <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Arrow" title="Kenneth Arrow">Kenneth Arrow</a>. Arrow, while working at the <a href="/wiki/RAND_Corporation" title="RAND Corporation">RAND Corporation</a>, had most famously shown that when voters have three or more distinct alternatives (options), any <a href="/wiki/Ranked_voting_system" title="Ranked voting system">ranked order</a> <a href="/wiki/Voting_system" title="Voting system">voting system</a> will in at least some situations inevitably conflict with what many assume to be basic democratic norms. Sen's contribution to the literature was to show under what conditions <a href="/wiki/Arrow%27s_impossibility_theorem" title="Arrow's impossibility theorem">Arrow's impossibility theorem</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> applied, as well as to extend and enrich the theory of social choice, informed by his interests in <a href="/wiki/History_of_economic_thought" title="History of economic thought">history of economic thought</a> and philosophy.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amartya_Sen_NIH.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="320" data-file-width="200" height="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e0/Amartya_Sen_NIH.jpg" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amartya_Sen_NIH.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Official Portrait at the Nobel Prize</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1981, Sen published <i>Poverty and Famines: An Essay on Entitlement and Deprivation</i> (1981), a book in which he argued that famine occurs not only from a lack of food, but from inequalities built into mechanisms for distributing food. Sen also argued that the Bengal famine was caused by an urban economic boom that raised food prices, thereby causing millions of rural workers to starve to death when their wages did not keep up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Time-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sen's interest in famine stemmed from personal experience. As a nine-year-old boy, he witnessed the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_famine_of_1943" title="Bengal famine of 1943">Bengal famine of 1943</a>, in which three million people perished. This staggering loss of life was unnecessary, Sen later concluded. He presents data that there was an adequate food supply in Bengal at the time, but particular groups of people including rural landless labourers and urban service providers like haircutters did not have the means to buy food as its price rose rapidly due to factors that include British military acquisition, <a href="/wiki/Panic_buying" title="Panic buying">panic buying</a>, hoarding, and <a href="/wiki/Price_gouging" title="Price gouging">price gouging</a>, all connected to the war in the region. In <i>Poverty and Famines</i>, Sen revealed that in many cases of famine, food supplies were not significantly reduced. In Bengal, for example, food production, while down on the previous year, was higher than in previous non-famine years. Thus, Sen points to a number of social and economic factors, such as declining wages, unemployment, rising food prices, and poor food-distribution, which led to starvation. His <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capabilities_approach" title="Capabilities approach">capabilities approach</a> focuses on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Positive_freedom" title="Positive freedom">positive freedom</a>, a person's actual ability to be or do something, rather than on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_freedom" title="Negative freedom">negative freedom</a> approaches, which are common in economics and simply focuses on non-interference. In the Bengal famine, rural laborers' negative freedom to buy food was not affected. However, they still starved because they were not positively free to do anything, they did not have the functioning of nourishment, nor the capability to escape morbidity.</p>
<p>In addition to his important work on the causes of famines, Sen's work in the field of development economics has had considerable influence in the formulation of the "<a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Report" title="Human Development Report">Human Development Report</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNDP_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNDP-16">[16]</a></sup> published by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Development_Programme" title="United Nations Development Programme">United Nations Development Programme</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> This annual publication that ranks countries on a variety of economic and social indicators owes much to the contributions by Sen among other social choice theorists in the area of economic measurement of poverty and inequality.</p>
<p>Sen's revolutionary contribution to development economics and social indicators is the concept of "<a href="/wiki/Capability_approach" title="Capability approach">capability</a>" developed in his article "Equality of What".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-What-18">[18]</a></sup> He argues that governments should be measured against the concrete capabilities of their citizens. This is because top-down development will always trump <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> as long as the definition of terms remains in doubt (is a "right" something that must be provided or something that simply cannot be taken away?). For instance, in the United States citizens have a hypothetical "right" to vote. To Sen, this concept is fairly empty. In order for citizens to have a capacity to vote, they first must have "functionings". These "functionings" can range from the very broad, such as the availability of education, to the very specific, such as transportation to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polling_station" title="Polling station">polls</a>. Only when such barriers are removed can the citizen truly be said to act out of personal choice. It is up to the individual society to make the list of minimum capabilities guaranteed by that society. For an example of the "capabilities approach" in practice, see <a href="/wiki/Martha_Nussbaum" title="Martha Nussbaum">Martha Nussbaum</a>'s <i>Women and Human Development</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>He wrote a controversial article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Review_of_Books" title="The New York Review of Books">The New York Review of Books</a></i> entitled "More Than 100 Million Women Are Missing" (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Missing_women_of_Asia" title="Missing women of Asia">Missing women of Asia</a>), analyzing the mortality impact of unequal rights between the genders in the developing world, particularly Asia. Other studies, including one by <a href="/wiki/Emily_Oster" title="Emily Oster">Emily Oster</a>, had argued that this is an overestimation, though Oster has since then recanted her conclusions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1998, Sen further advanced and redefined the capability approach in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Development_as_Freedom" title="Development as Freedom">Development as Freedom</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Sen argues that development should be viewed as an effort to advance the real freedoms that individuals enjoy, rather than simply focusing on metrics such as GDP or income-per-capita. Sen was inspired by violent acts he had witnessed as a child leading up to the Partition of India in 1947. On one morning, a Muslim laborer named Kader Mia stumbled through the rear gate of Sen's family home, bleeding from a knife wound in his back. Because of his extreme poverty, he had come to Sen's primarily Hindu neighborhood searching for work; his choices were the starvation of his family or the risk of death in coming to the neighborhood. The price of Kader Mia's economic unfreedom was his death. This experience led Sen to begin thinking about economic unfreedom from a young age.</p>
<p>In <i>Development as Freedom</i>, Sen outlines five specific types of freedoms: political freedoms, economic facilities, social opportunities, transparency guarantees, and protective security. Political freedoms, the first of these, refers to the ability of the people to have a voice in government and to be able to scrutinize the authorities. Economic facilities concern both the resources within the market and the market mechanism itself. Any focus on income and wealth in the country would serve to increase the economic facilities for the people. Social opportunities deal with the establishments that provide benefits like healthcare or education for the populace, allowing individuals to live better lives. Transparency guarantees allow individuals to interact with some degree of trust and knowledge of the interaction. Protective security is the system of social safety nets that prevent a group affected by poverty being subjected to terrible misery. Before Sen's work, these had been viewed as only the ends of development; luxuries afforded to countries that focus on increasing income. However, Sen argues that the increase in real freedoms should be both the ends and the means of development.</p>
<p>Welfare economics seeks to evaluate economic policies in terms of their effects on the well-being of the community. Sen, who devoted his career to such issues, was called the "conscience of his profession". His influential monograph <i>Collective Choice and Social Welfare</i> (1970), which addressed problems related to individual rights (including formulation of the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_paradox" title="Liberal paradox">liberal paradox</a>), justice and equity, majority rule, and the availability of information about individual conditions, inspired researchers to turn their attention to issues of basic welfare. Sen devised methods of measuring poverty that yielded useful information for improving economic conditions for the poor. For instance, his theoretical work on inequality provided an explanation for why there are fewer women than men in India<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> and China despite the fact that in the West and in poor but medically unbiased countries, women have lower <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rates</a> at all ages, live longer, and make a slight majority of the population. Sen claimed that this skewed ratio results from the better health treatment and childhood opportunities afforded boys in those countries, as well as <a href="/wiki/Sex-selective_abortion" title="Sex-selective abortion">sex-selective abortions</a>.</p>
<p>Governments and international organizations handling food crises were influenced by Sen's work. His views encouraged policy makers to pay attention not only to alleviating immediate suffering but also to finding ways to replace the lost income of the poor—for example through public works—and to maintain stable prices for food. A vigorous defender of political freedom, Sen believed that famines do not occur in functioning democracies because their leaders must be more responsive to the demands of the citizens. In order for economic growth to be achieved, he argued, social reforms—such as improvements in education and public health—must precede economic reform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2009, Sen published a book called <i>The Idea of Justice</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_2010_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_2010-1">[1]</a></sup> Based on his previous work in welfare economics and social choice theory, but also on his philosophical thoughts, he presented his own theory of justice that he meant to be an alternative to the influential modern theories of justice of <a href="/wiki/John_Rawls" title="John Rawls">John Rawls</a> or <a href="/wiki/John_Harsanyi" title="John Harsanyi">John Harsanyi</a>. In opposition to Rawls but also earlier justice theoreticians <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a> or <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a>, and inspired by the philosophical works of <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mary_Wollstonecraft" title="Mary Wollstonecraft">Mary Wollstonecraft</a>, Sen developed a theory that is both comparative and realizations-oriented (instead of being transcendental and institutional). However, he still regards institutions and processes as being important. As an alternative to Rawls's <a href="/wiki/Veil_of_ignorance" title="Veil of ignorance">veil of ignorance</a>, Sen chose the <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">thought experiment</a> of an impartial spectator as the basis of his theory of justice. He also stressed the importance of public discussion (understanding democracy in the sense of <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>) and a focus on people's capabilities (an <a href="/wiki/Capability_approach" title="Capability approach">approach</a> that he had co-developed), including the notion of universal human rights, in evaluating various states with regard to justice.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Professional_career">Professional career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amartya_Sen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Professional career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sen began his career both as a teacher and a research scholar in the Department of Economics, <a href="/wiki/Jadavpur_University" title="Jadavpur University">Jadavpur University</a>. Between 1960 and 1961, Sen was a visiting Professor at <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> in the United States, where he got to know <a href="/wiki/Paul_Samuelson" title="Paul Samuelson">Paul Samuelson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Solow" title="Robert Solow">Robert Solow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Franco_Modigliani" title="Franco Modigliani">Franco Modigliani</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Norbert_Wiener" title="Norbert Wiener">Norbert Wiener</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Calcutta_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Calcutta-24">[24]</a></sup> He was also a visiting Professor at UC-Berkeley and <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell</a>. He taught as Professor of Economics between 1963 and 1971 at the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_School_of_Economics" title="Delhi School of Economics">Delhi School of Economics</a> (where he completed his <i>magnum opus</i> Collective Choice and Social Welfare in 1969).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Delhi_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Delhi-25">[25]</a></sup> During this time he was also a frequent visitor to various other premiere Indian economic schools and centres of excellence like <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru_University,_Delhi" title="Jawaharlal Nehru University, Delhi">Jawaharlal Nehru University</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Statistical_Institute" title="Indian Statistical Institute">Indian Statistical Institute</a>, <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Development_Studies" title="Centre for Development Studies">Centre for Development Studies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gokhale_Institute_of_Politics_and_Economics" title="Gokhale Institute of Politics and Economics">Gokhale Institute of Politics and Economics</a> and Centre for Studies in Social Sciences. Sen was a companion of distinguished economists like <a href="/wiki/Manmohan_Singh" title="Manmohan Singh">Manmohan Singh</a> (Ex-Prime Minister of India and a veteran economist responsible for liberalizing the Indian economy), <a href="/wiki/K._N._Raj" title="K. N. Raj">K. N. Raj</a> (Advisor to various Prime Ministers and a veteran economist who was the founder of <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Development_Studies" title="Centre for Development Studies">Centre for Development Studies</a>, Trivandrum, which is one of India's premier think tanks and schools) and <a href="/wiki/Jagdish_Bhagwati" title="Jagdish Bhagwati">Jagdish Bhagwati</a> (who is known to be one of the greatest Indian economists in the field of International Trade and currently teaches at <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>). This is a period considered to be a Golden Period in the history of DSE. In 1972, he joined the <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a> as a Professor of Economics where he taught until 1977. From 1977 to 1986 he taught at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>, where he was first a Professor of Economics and Fellow of <a href="/wiki/Nuffield_College,_Oxford" title="Nuffield College, Oxford">Nuffield College</a>, and then the Drummond Professor of Political Economy and a Fellow of <a href="/wiki/All_Souls_College,_Oxford" title="All Souls College, Oxford">All Souls College</a> from 1980. In 1987, he joined <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard</a> as the Thomas W. Lamont University Professor of Economics. In 1998 he was appointed as Master of Trinity College, Cambridge,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Master_Camb_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Master_Camb-8">[8]</a></sup> becoming the first Asian head of an <a href="/wiki/Oxbridge" title="Oxbridge">Oxbridge</a> college.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In January 2004, Sen returned to Harvard. He also established the Eva Colorni Trust at the former <a href="/wiki/London_Guildhall_University" title="London Guildhall University">London Guildhall University</a> in the name of his deceased wife.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nalanda_Project">Nalanda Project</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amartya_Sen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Nalanda Project">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In May 2007, he was appointed as chairman<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_Nalanda_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_Nalanda-27">[27]</a></sup> of Nalanda Mentor Group to examine the framework of international cooperation, and proposed structure of partnership, which would govern the establishment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nalanda_International_University" title="Nalanda International University">Nalanda International University</a> Project as an international centre of education seeking to revive the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_center_of_higher_learning" title="Ancient center of higher learning">ancient center of higher learning</a> which was present in India from the 5th century to 1197.</p>
<p>On 19 July 2012, Sen was named the first chancellor of the proposed Nalanda University (NU).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Teaching began in August 2014. On 20 February 2015, Amartya Sen withdrew his candidature for a second term.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership_and_associations">Membership and associations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amartya_Sen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Membership and associations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>He has served as president of the <a href="/wiki/Econometric_Society" title="Econometric Society">Econometric Society</a> (1984), the International Economic Association (1986–1989), the Indian Economic Association (1989) and the <a href="/wiki/American_Economic_Association" title="American Economic Association">American Economic Association</a> (1994). He has also served as President of the <a href="/wiki/Development_Studies_Association" title="Development Studies Association">Development Studies Association</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_and_Capability_Association" title="Human Development and Capability Association">Human Development and Capability Association</a>. He serves as the Chair of the International Advisory Board of the Center for Human and Economic Development Studies at <a href="/wiki/Peking_University" title="Peking University">Peking University</a> in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sen has been called "the Conscience of the profession" and "the <a href="/wiki/Mother_Teresa" title="Mother Teresa">Mother Teresa</a> of Economics"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_Profile_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_Profile-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> for his work on famine, <a href="/wiki/Human_development_theory" title="Human development theory">human development theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Welfare_economics" title="Welfare economics">welfare economics</a>, the underlying mechanisms of poverty, <a href="/wiki/Gender_inequality" title="Gender inequality">gender inequality</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Contributions_to_liberal_theory" title="Contributions to liberal theory">political liberalism</a>. However, he denies the comparison to Mother Teresa, saying that he has never tried to follow a lifestyle of dedicated self-sacrifice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Amartya Sen also added his voice to the campaign against the anti-gay <a href="/wiki/Section_377_of_the_Indian_Penal_Code" title="Section 377 of the Indian Penal Code">Section 377 of the Indian Penal Code</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sen is also a member of the <a href="/wiki/Berggruen_Institute" title="Berggruen Institute">Berggruen Institute</a>'s 21st Century Council.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media_and_culture">Media and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amartya_Sen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Media and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A 57-minute documentary named <i>Amartya Sen: A Life Re-examined</i> directed by <a href="/wiki/Suman_Ghosh" title="Suman Ghosh">Suman Ghosh</a> details his life and work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_Documentary_Ghosh_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_Documentary_Ghosh-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Documentary_Sen_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Documentary_Sen-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2001 portrait of Sen by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Annabel_Cullen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Annabel Cullen (page does not exist)">Annabel Cullen</a> is in Trinity College's collection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> A 2003 portrait of Sen hangs in the <a href="/wiki/National_Portrait_Gallery,_London" title="National Portrait Gallery, London">National Portrait Gallery</a> in London.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><br/>
In 2011, he was present at the <i>Rabindra Utsab</i> ceremony at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bangabandhu_International_Conference_Centre" title="Bangabandhu International Conference Centre">Bangabandhu International Conference Centre</a> (BICC), <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>. He unveiled the cover of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sruti_Gitobitan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sruti Gitobitan (page does not exist)">Sruti Gitobitan</a>, a Rabindrasangeet album comprising all the 2222 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tagore_songs" title="Tagore songs">Tagore songs</a>, brought out by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rezwana_Chowdhury_Bannya" title="Rezwana Chowdhury Bannya">Rezwana Chowdhury Bannya</a>, principal of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Shurer_Dhara_School_of_Music&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Shurer Dhara School of Music (page does not exist)">Shurer Dhara School of Music</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amartya_Sen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Amartya Sen was critical of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Minister" title="Prime Minister">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Narendra_Modi" title="Narendra Modi">Narendra Modi</a> when he was announced as the prime ministerial candidate by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BJP" title="BJP">BJP</a>. In April 2014, he said that Modi would not make a good Prime Minister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> But later in December 2014, he changed his views and said that Narendra Modi did give people a sense of faith that things can happen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In February 2015, Sen opted out of seeking a second term for the chancellor post of Nalanda university stating that the Government of India was not keen on him continuing in the post.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life_and_beliefs">Personal life and beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amartya_Sen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Personal life and beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sen has been married three times. His first wife was <a href="/wiki/Nabaneeta_Dev_Sen" title="Nabaneeta Dev Sen">Nabaneeta Dev Sen</a>, an Indian writer and scholar, by whom he had two daughters: <a href="/wiki/Antara_Dev_Sen" title="Antara Dev Sen">Antara</a>, a journalist and publisher, and <a href="/wiki/Nandana_Sen" title="Nandana Sen">Nandana</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Bollywood" title="Bollywood">Bollywood</a> actress. Their marriage broke up shortly after they moved to London in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_Profile_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_Profile-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1978 Sen married Eva Colorni, an Italian economist, and the couple had two children, a daughter Indrani, who is a journalist in New York, and a son Kabir, a hip hop artist, MC, and music teacher at <a href="/wiki/Shady_Hill_School" title="Shady Hill School">Shady Hill School</a>. Eva died of cancer in 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_Profile_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_Profile-30">[30]</a></sup> In 1991, Sen married <a href="/wiki/Emma_Georgina_Rothschild" title="Emma Georgina Rothschild">Emma Georgina Rothschild</a>, who serves as the Jeremy and Jane Knowles Professor of History at Harvard University.</p>
<p>The Sens have a house in <a href="/wiki/Cambridge,_Massachusetts" title="Cambridge, Massachusetts">Cambridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, which is the base from which they teach during the academic year. They also have a home in Cambridge, England, where Sen is a Fellow of Trinity College, Cambridge, and Rothschild is a Fellow of <a href="/wiki/Magdalene_College,_Cambridge" title="Magdalene College, Cambridge">Magdalene College</a>. He usually spends his winter holidays at his home in <a href="/wiki/Santiniketan" title="Santiniketan">Santiniketan</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>, India, where he used to go on long bike rides until recently. Asked how he relaxes, he replies: "I read a lot and like arguing with people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sen_Profile_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sen_Profile-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sen is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheist" title="Atheist">atheist</a> and holds that this can be associated with <a href="/wiki/Atheism_in_Hinduism" title="Atheism in Hinduism">one of the atheist schools in Hinduism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Charvaka" title="Charvaka">Lokayata</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In an interview for the magazine <i>California</i>, which is published by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Berkeley" title="University of California, Berkeley">University of California, Berkeley</a>, he noted:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In some ways people had got used to the idea that India was spiritual and religion-oriented. That gave a leg up to the religious interpretation of India, despite the fact that <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> had a larger <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheistic" title="Atheistic">atheistic</a> literature than exists in any other <a href="/wiki/Classical_language" title="Classical language">classical language</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vidyaranya" title="Vidyaranya">Madhava Acharya</a>, the remarkable 14th century philosopher, wrote this rather great book called <i>Sarvadarshansamgraha</i>, which discussed all the religious schools of thought within the Hindu structure. The first chapter is “Atheism”—a very strong presentation of the argument in favor of <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">atheism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>.</p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honours">Awards and honours</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Amartya_Sen&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Awards and honours">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sen has received over 90 <a href="/wiki/Honorary_degree" title="Honorary degree">honorary degrees</a> from universities around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith_Prize" title="Adam Smith Prize">Adam Smith Prize</a>, 1954</li>
<li>Foreign Honorary Member of the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Arts_and_Sciences" title="American Academy of Arts and Sciences">American Academy of Arts and Sciences</a>, 1981<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAAS_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS-48">[48]</a></sup></li>
<li>Honorary fellowship by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Institute_of_Social_Studies" title="Institute of Social Studies">Institute of Social Studies</a>, 1984</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nobel_Memorial_Prize_in_Economic_Sciences" title="Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences">Nobel Memorial Prize in Economic Sciences</a>, 1998</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bharat_Ratna" title="Bharat Ratna">Bharat Ratna</a>, the highest civilian award in India, 1999</li>
<li>Honorary citizenship of <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a>, 1999</li>
<li>Order of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Companion_of_Honour" title="Companion of Honour">Companion of Honour</a>, UK, 2000</li>
<li>Leontief Prize, 2000</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Fellowships#The_Eisenhower_Medal_for_Leadership_and_Service" title="Eisenhower Fellowships">Eisenhower Medal for Leadership and Service</a>, 2000</li>
<li>351st Commencement Speaker of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, 2001</li>
<li>International Humanist Award from the <a href="/wiki/International_Humanist_and_Ethical_Union" title="International Humanist and Ethical Union">International Humanist and Ethical Union</a>, 2002</li>
<li>Lifetime Achievement Award by the Indian Chamber of Commerce, 2004</li>
<li>Life Time Achievement award by Bangkok-based <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Economic_and_Social_Commission_for_Asia_and_the_Pacific" title="United Nations Economic and Social Commission for Asia and the Pacific">United Nations Economic and Social Commission for Asia and the Pacific</a> (UNESCAP)</li>
<li>Honorary degree, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pavia" title="University of Pavia">University of Pavia</a>, 2005</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/National_Humanities_Medal" title="National Humanities Medal">National Humanities Medal</a>, 2011</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Aztec_Eagle" title="Order of the Aztec Eagle">Order of the Aztec Eagle</a>, 2012<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></li>
<li>Commander of the French <a href="/wiki/Legion_of_Honour" title="Legion of Honour">Legion of Honour</a>, 2013<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></li>
<li>25 Greatest Global Living Legends In India by NDTV, 2013<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></li>
<li>Top 100 thinkers who have defined our century by <a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a>, 2014</li>
<li>Charleston-EFG John Maynard Keynes Prize, 2015<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
